  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/704688-i-have-a-question-about-a-certain-aspect">I have a question about a certain aspect of this book, namely the magic. In the description it says something about &quot;old-world magic&quot;. I can guess what it means, but I&#39;m slightly scared it&#39;ll turn out to be about a spellcasting Merlin - like in a Lord of the Rings novel or something. Is this the case? Or is it more of a real, genuine account of the age of Arthur (I hope so!)?Thanks!</a>
</div>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>

</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/51831148-bryan-forsyth">Bryan Forsyth</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">The 'magic' is purely in the minds of the characters. Its source is superstition, irrationality, ignorance, and religion.<br/><br/>You will not see thunderbolts<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">The 'magic' is purely in the minds of the characters. Its source is superstition, irrationality, ignorance, and religion.<br /><br />You will not see thunderbolts fly from staffs or supernatural healing of deadly wounds.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1945181511" />
    <a title="Petrik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik"><img alt="Petrik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568016775p2/59458347.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1945181511?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petrik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petrik" href="/user/show/59458347-petrik">Petrik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781405928328" href="/book/show/30171946-the-winter-king">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Historical fiction &amp; Arthurian fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Petrik by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/6696780-john-gwynne">John Gwynne</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1945181511" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17716035617129420180"><b>4.5/5 Stars<br/><br/>Depending on the rest of the trilogy, this could be the most original and the best Arthurian legend retelling of all time, out of all medium.</b><br/><br/>A little background before I start my review; this is my first dive into Bernard Cornwell’s work and only my second time reading a historical fiction, so this is totally out of my comfort read but I’m delighted with my decision to go out of my usual read. I’ve heard of the name Bernard Cornwell several times until now, all pretty much claimed he’</span>
  <span id="freeText17716035617129420180" style="display:none"><b>4.5/5 Stars<br><br>Depending on the rest of the trilogy, this could be the most original and the best Arthurian legend retelling of all time, out of all medium.</b><br><br>A little background before I start my review; this is my first dive into Bernard Cornwell’s work and only my second time reading a historical fiction, so this is totally out of my comfort read but I’m delighted with my decision to go out of my usual read. I’ve heard of the name Bernard Cornwell several times until now, all pretty much claimed he’s a legend in ‘Historical Fiction’ genre but nothing ever truly pushed me into starting his books until last March when I finished binge reading the entire ‘The Faithful and the Fallen’ series. Both the series and the author <i>(John Gwynne)</i> since then have been included in my favorites of all time lists. I decided back then to do an interview with Gwynne and one of my questions was:<br><br><b>“If you have to recommend one book or series for everyone, what came into mind and why?” </b><br><br>His answer was 'The Warlord Chronicles' by Bernard Cornwell and that’s how I stumbled upon this series, and how I finally decided to give his work a try. Click this link for my full interview with Gwynne for anyone who’s interested. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://booknest.eu/component/k2/30-blog/391-interviewjohngwynne">http://booknest.eu/component/k2/30-bl...</a><br><br>On to the review, even though this is still only the first book out of a trilogy, I can already see why Cornwell is named as a legend in the genre. He managed to make my most disliked narrative, omniscient narrative into something that worked wonderfully.<br><br>Told in the similar style with Kvothe from Kingkiller Chronicles, we follow Derfel Cadarn, the main character, and the narrator, now old and a monk, recounts his journey with Arthur, his best friend, The King that Never Was, The Enemy of God and The Lord of Battles.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“The bards sing of love, they celebrate slaughter, they extol kings and flatter queens, but were I a poet I would write in praise of friendship.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>Most of the stories told here took place in the past, going back to the present times only five times in total throughout the entire book. This also means that Derfel pretty much knows all the events that will happen already during his narration and he reminded us over and over again about this with sentences like “it’s not until later that I find out what he meant”. This usually doesn’t work in my fantasy read but damn it worked so well in this story.<br><br>The Winter King mostly focused on Arthur’s struggle to unite Britain during the Dark Ages in the midst of Saxon’s inevitable invasion. Cornwell’s retelling of Arthur is magnificent, contrary to usual Arthurian legend; Cornwell erased every magical aspect, at least here anyway. Sure there’s a hint of magic in the world but they’re not actual magic per se, just superstitions that the population back then heavily believed. Cornwell has stated that The Winter King is a tale of the Dark Ages in which legend and imagination must compensate for the lack of historical records, as there’s no conclusive evidence on Arthur’s legend and he did it with greatness.<br><br>Arthurian legend has always been one of my favorite retellings, it’s been done countless times already in any medium but I’ve never once experienced a retelling as original and fantastic as this one. Cornwell’s storytelling and prose qualities are top notches. So many emotions were felt and delivered throughout my times reading this, thought provoking and realistically poignant such as this<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“And at the end of life, what does it all matter? We grow old and the young look at us and can never see that once we made a kingdom ring for love.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br> <br>or philosophical like this:<br><br> <b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>“But fate, as Merlin always taught us, is inexorable. Life is a jest of the Gods, Merlin liked to claim, and there is no justice. You must learn to laugh, he once told me, or else you'll just weep yourself to death.”</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>Not only the storytelling and prose are fantastic, Cornwell’s versions of the characters that we’ve known in the legend are very unique. Arthur, in particular, is amazing, felt like a real person that truly existed in the past despite this being written as a historical fiction. Also, a huge plus in originality towards Lancelot and Guinevere, for they have completely take on a direction that I never thought I would ever see in their character.<br><br>Do note however that this is a slow paced book, we only get a little taste of Cornwell’s big battle scenes <i>(another factor that he’s highly praised for)</i> in the last 60 pages of the book, if you love Shield-Wall, you’re going to love the battle scenes for sure.<br><br>Honestly, this could’ve been an easy 5-star book for me if it wasn’t for the first half of the book. The minor con I had with the book is that during the first half, the pages are very dense, a paragraph could last an entire page, with a minimum amount of dialogues. To give you a clear picture of what I’m talking about, here’s a picture of a paragraph I took from the book, non-spoiler of course<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1499426682i/23230716._SY540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The first half of the book mostly looked like that, as you can see, there’s almost no heavy dialogue section and this means you’re going to have to read tons of details and descriptions. Plus, the long chapters <i>(15 chapters for 490 pages)</i> made this book not an easy read. I felt my progress reading this book became very slow because of these situations. These can be a good or bad thing depending on your preferences but personally, I prefer it to be balanced. The second half however fixed this problem.<br><br>Overall, I truly enjoyed reading The Winter King and I thank John Gwynne for recommending this book to me. I will definitely continue with this trilogy and I highly recommend it to any fans of historical fiction and Arthurian legend.<br><br><b>Bonus Picture: My beautiful editions of The Warlord Chronicles</b><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1499106473i/23192159._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>You can find <a href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/26-pat-reviews/627-thewinterking" rel="nofollow noopener">this</a> and the rest of my Adult Epic/High Fantasy &amp; Sci-Fi reviews at <a href="http://booknest.eu/review/patrick" rel="nofollow noopener">BookNest</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17716035617129420180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2970645888" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2970645888" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63168028-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516912354p2/63168028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2970645888?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/63168028-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780140231861" href="/book/show/826595.The_Winter_King">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2970645888" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9085561381385997555"><b>Nascency</b><br/>The tale of King Arthur, Guinevere and Lancelot, the Knights of the Round Table, Sir Galahad and his quest for the Holy Grail, Excalibur, Merlin and his sorcery, and the age of chivalry are the ingredients of medieval fantasy and folklore. Bernard Cornwell writes his account which feels the most authentic version I’ve encountered and turns many of these former images on their head. The resulting novel creates an imagined tale that feels legitimate and historical.<br/><br/>The story is told in the </span>
  <span id="freeText9085561381385997555" style="display:none"><b>Nascency</b><br />The tale of King Arthur, Guinevere and Lancelot, the Knights of the Round Table, Sir Galahad and his quest for the Holy Grail, Excalibur, Merlin and his sorcery, and the age of chivalry are the ingredients of medieval fantasy and folklore. Bernard Cornwell writes his account which feels the most authentic version I’ve encountered and turns many of these former images on their head. The resulting novel creates an imagined tale that feels legitimate and historical.<br /><br />The story is told in the first person looking back in time, from the perspective of Dervel, a soldier and monk, who fought at Arthur’s side. As he starts to write his account, his loyalty to Arthur is apparent. <blockquote>
  <i> “These are the tales of Arthur, the Warlord, the King that Never Was, the Enemy of God and, may the living Christ and Bishop Sansum forgive me, the best man I ever knew. How I have wept for Arthur.” </i>
</blockquote> The wonderful opportunity this structure provides is that the story of Arthur can be told from an onlooker seeing the positive and negative attribute of Arthur, his wins and his mistakes through a lens with less emotion and the benefit of distance. It also enables the location and existence of a lowly person to be painted in beautiful sensory detail, and the issues he faced dealing with many of the extensive iconic characters in this story.<br /><br />The period is set in the late fifth century, shortly after the Romans left Briton, and the country is split into several regions each ruled by their own king. The Saxon conflicts are escalating and war from every side is commonplace. Cornwell creates an amazing atmosphere of medieval Briton that permeates through every aspect of the novel. The politics and machinations between the multiple power-hungry and warrior leaders are deep-rooted and persistent and every engagement is judged with caution and an expectation that ruthless and instant changes can and will occur. It is also a period where priests and druids battle with the conflict of Christianity and the old gods. The era of Merlin, magic and sorcery is coming to an end but they still hold considerable influence.<br /><br />Arthur is the bastard son of Uther the Pendragon, very accomplished in battle, although not always given the credit. Uther’s legitimate grandson, Mordred (The Winter King), has a twisted foot which is taken as a bad omen. It's not the only thing that's twisted as he's a ruthless, evil and unforgiving personality that he doesn’t hide and unfortunately with an astute and cunning mind, may become a formidable force. Arthur still upholds his allegiance to Mordred, even though his vision of peace and justice is a polar opposite.<br /><br />Bernard Cornwell claimed that this trilogy was his favourite and best piece of work. I haven’t read enough of his other works to make that comparison but if they’re better than this – well I have a lot to look forward to.<br /><br />This is an outstanding example of the historical fiction genre and the best take on the iconic Arthur story I’ve read or watched. I would highly recommend this book and series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9085561381385997555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_553838" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/553838" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47809-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175607989p2/47809.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/553838?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/47809-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">see review</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47809-ryan?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47809-ryan?shelf=arthurian">arthurian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer553838" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9637058051866123404">I really can't say enough about this book. There are a lot of reasons to enjoy books and this one scores highest in so many categories. It is just very fun to read.<br/><br/><b>Who would I recommend this book to?</b><br/><b>If you loved</b> The <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33.The_Lord_of_the_Rings__The_Lord_of_the_Rings___1_3_' title='The Lord of the Rings (The Lord of the Rings, #1-3) by J.R.R. Tolkien' rel='nofollow noopener'> Lord of the Rings</a> but the smallest part of you that doesn't care about poetry kind of wished it had a little more action . . .<br/><b>If you loved</b> watching the movie <b>Braveheart</b> but wish it was a little more accurate historically . . .<br/><b>If you were excited</b> about the 2004 movie <b>King Arthur</b>, whi</span>
  <span id="freeText9637058051866123404" style="display:none">I really can't say enough about this book. There are a lot of reasons to enjoy books and this one scores highest in so many categories. It is just very fun to read.<br /><br /><b>Who would I recommend this book to?</b><br /><b>If you loved</b> The <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33.The_Lord_of_the_Rings__The_Lord_of_the_Rings___1_3_" title="The Lord of the Rings (The Lord of the Rings, #1-3) by J.R.R. Tolkien" rel="nofollow noopener"> Lord of the Rings</a> but the smallest part of you that doesn't care about poetry kind of wished it had a little more action . . .<br /><b>If you loved</b> watching the movie <b>Braveheart</b> but wish it was a little more accurate historically . . .<br /><b>If you were excited</b> about the 2004 movie <b>King Arthur</b>, which although was advertised to be "the real story of King Arthur" had Arthur and his band of Mongolians defending the <i>Scottish</i> border against <i>Saxons</i>. The hell kind of sense does that make? Saxons invaded the South west coast, the region of England now called Saxony, the people Arthur would have fought on the Scottish border were, yes, you guessed it <i>Scotts</i> . . . <br /><b>If you were excited</b> ditto <b>Tristan and Isolde</b> . . .<br /><b>If you loved</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6252.Robert_Jordan" title="Robert Jordan" rel="nofollow noopener"> Robert Jordan</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/228665.The_Eye_of_the_World__Wheel_of_Time___1_" title="The Eye of the World (Wheel of Time, #1) by Robert Jordan" rel="nofollow noopener"> Wheel of Time</a> series but don't have the eons required to read each with a 1000+ pages per volume . . .<br /><br />I'll break down the idea behind the book. Yes, it's about King Arthur. And I know what you are thinking, "it's a magic book". <b>No!</b> there are no fairies, no pixie dust, no <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82192.The_Crystal_Cave__Arthurian_Saga___1_" title="The Crystal Cave (Arthurian Saga, #1) by Mary Stewart" rel="nofollow noopener"> Crystal Cave</a> (sorry <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15590.Mary_Stewart" title="Mary Stewart" rel="nofollow noopener"> Mary Stewart</a> I loved your books too, but for slightly different reasons). The book does a real, <i>researched</i> job in telling a story which might have happened. Many of the characters believe in magic and things happen which might be interpreted as such but they might also be explained by realistic means.<br /><br />It portrays Arthur as a warlord in around 500 AD right after the Romans abandoned their foothold in the British Isles and right before the Anglo-Saxon invasion turned the Celtic land of Britain into Angl-land (England). History indicates something stopped the Saxon invasion for about 50 years and most historians believe that might have been Arthur. There is also no jousting (a sport popular about 700 years later). The story is different from the traditional Arthur Legend but similar enough to make it enjoyable to people who also liked:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/82192.The_Crystal_Cave__Arthurian_Saga___1_" title="The Crystal Cave (Arthurian Saga, #1) by Mary Stewart" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Crystal Cave</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43545.The_Once_and_Future_King__The_Once_and_Future_King__1_4_" title="The Once and Future King (The Once and Future King #1-4) by T.H. White" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Once and Future King</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/219269.Le_Morte_d_Arthur__Vol__2" title="Le Morte d'Arthur, Vol. 2 by Thomas Malory" rel="nofollow noopener"> Le Morte d'Arthur</a><br />and the many hundreds of others, film and book.<br /><br />Oh, and I don't know who wrote the description which accompanies this book (probably someone at [<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.amazon.com">www.amazon.com</a>] but they say it is written in "flat American diction". I don't believe <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell" title="Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener"> Bernard Cornwell</a>, a native of Britain, would appreciate that. He might live in America currently but is still quite English. What they may have meant to say is that it is "readable". Why they didn't just say that I have no idea.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9637058051866123404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1945202321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1945202321" />
    <a title="Celeste" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste"><img alt="Celeste" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481055226p2/7317186.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1945202321?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Celeste" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Celeste" href="/user/show/7317186-celeste">Celeste</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7317186-celeste?shelf=historical">historical</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1945202321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17602344340911951831"><b>Full review now posted!<br/>Original review can be found at <a href='http://booknest.eu' rel='nofollow noopener'>Booknest</a>.</b><br/><br/>Here lies a book that didn’t enthrall me, but somehow fascinated me. I wasn’t filled with longing to pick it up and continue reading, but every time I did I was given incredibly interesting theories and historical information. This was likely the most probable telling of the Arthurian legend that I’ve come across. The mythos of Arthur and Merlin and Excalibur and Camelot has always intrigued me, but it’s always remained in the realm</span>
  <span id="freeText17602344340911951831" style="display:none"><b>Full review now posted!<br />Original review can be found at <a href="http://booknest.eu" rel="nofollow noopener">Booknest</a>.</b><br /><br />Here lies a book that didn’t enthrall me, but somehow fascinated me. I wasn’t filled with longing to pick it up and continue reading, but every time I did I was given incredibly interesting theories and historical information. This was likely the most probable telling of the Arthurian legend that I’ve come across. The mythos of Arthur and Merlin and Excalibur and Camelot has always intrigued me, but it’s always remained in the realm of myth. For the first time in my life, I read something that convinced me of the possibility of Arthur’s existence. Not its likelihood, mind you, but its possibility, which is still an astonishing change for me regarding a myth.<br /><br />Cornwell sets his tale in the 5th century, after the Roman occupation of Britain has ended. Saxons are invading and Britons are warring amongst themselves. This is a land of warring factions and a multitude of kings, and this is where Cornwell has planted his version of Arthur. Here, Arthur’s tale is told by Brother Derfel, an aging monk who wasn’t always a Christian. In his youth, Derfel was a pagan and a warrior who fought alongside Arthur. In the framework of Cornwall’s story, Derfel is writing out the true story of Arthur for Igraine, the young Queen over the realm that houses the monastery. Witnessing Arthur’s story from an eyewitness, and one who isn’t one of the names we’re familiar with, was a unique perspective. And trying to reconcile Derfel himself, the aging Christian monk and the young pagan warrior, is actually one of my favorite aspects of the novel. How radically people can change always intrigues me.<br /><br />There were some people Cornwell portrayed here that were at complete odds with almost everything I’ve ever read or heard. Particularly, his representation of Guinevere and Lancelot. Even though they were pretty people, neither of them seemed to have much goodness within them. Guinevere here is a catalyst for war, much like Helen of Troy. I will never be able to fathom shattering a kingdom in the name of love, though I know it’s one of the most ancient of justifications for declarations of war. And Lancelot is just awful, though I’m still not sure how many of his failings are truth and how many are exaggerated through the eyes of our narrator. However, a hero he is not, though he knows how to twist events in the minds of poets to ensure his legacy.<br /><br />The presentation of the Druid belief system was my other favorite aspect of this book. Their superstitions and “spell casting” were absolutely fascinating. And disturbing. I don’t think I’ve ever read another book that contained this much spitting. Or urine-flinging. Or cow dung as hair product. And the Druid’s view of the Christian interlopers, and the way those opposing faiths both widened the rift in their land and how the people banded together in battle in spite of those opposing faiths, was captivating. One of the main reasons I’ll be continuing the trilogy at some later date is to understand how Derfel transitioned from one faith to the other.<br /><br />Even though I have a deep appreciation for both the story and Cornwell’s writing, I have to admit that I struggled reading this. It was just so dense. The information was interesting, for sure, but sometimes I felt so glutted by the outpouring of information that I couldn’t digest quickly enough to keep reading. Cornwell did an insane amount of research, and it really shows. I feel like I learned so much about the Druid faith and ancient Britain, but that learning sometimes overwhelmed the story. The book reminded me of some of the really amazing history books I read in college. Well written and fascinating, but too dense to read for simple enjoyment. Also, it felt a little like the vast majority of the book was either preparing for battle, engaging in battle, or the aftermath of battle. Which is fine, but is something I get really bogged down in.<br /><br />If you love historical fiction, this is definitely the book for you. If you’re obsessed with Arthurian legend, you owe it to yourself to give this a read. And if you just can’t get enough of battlefields, I think I found your new favorite book.<br /><br />I read this because <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/59458347-petrik" rel="nofollow noopener">Petrik</a> loved the series so much.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17602344340911951831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_929520046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/929520046" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/929520046?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780140231861" href="/book/show/826595.The_Winter_King">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=4-star-reads">4-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer929520046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17910171777263351609">Nobody does this quite as well as Bernard Cornwell. He is quite literally the master of this genre. He creates a vivid warrior culture time and time again, and I will never get bored of it. This is saying a lot because Bernard Cornwell has written a huge amount of novels over the years and a few are similar in ways, but I don’t care because they’re just so good. This time Bernard Cornwell tells the story of Arthur, though not from the perspective of Arthur; he tells it from the point of view of </span>
  <span id="freeText17910171777263351609" style="display:none">Nobody does this quite as well as Bernard Cornwell. He is quite literally the master of this genre. He creates a vivid warrior culture time and time again, and I will never get bored of it. This is saying a lot because Bernard Cornwell has written a huge amount of novels over the years and a few are similar in ways, but I don’t care because they’re just so good. This time Bernard Cornwell tells the story of Arthur, though not from the perspective of Arthur; he tells it from the point of view of one of his footman. <br><br><b> An interesting take on the Arthurian myth </b><br><br>Derfel is a spearman in the service of Arthur and he narrates Arthur’s story. I’m glad that we weren’t privy to Arthur’s thoughts and emotion because it helped to create an idea about him being an untouchable being. What I mean is that Arthur is better than the common man, and by telling the story like this it assumes a sense of distance between someone like Derfel, who in himself is honourable, and someone who is, in essence, a man beyond measure. It created an Arthur that was as enigmatic as he was courageous and noble. I think it was a great idea. <br><br>This is an awakening change to what I thought would be the predictable route of a novel like this. We see the events, and people, that surround Arthur’s life from a different perspective. One that holds Arthur in high esteem, but in spite of this, we also see the mistakes Arthur makes as a commander. He takes the wrong bride in a moment of selfish passion and almost dooms himself in the process. I emphasise the word mistakes because this is not the usual Camelot fairy tale; this is a gritty realistic approach to the legend. <br><br><b> More historical than legend </b><br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/photos/1439539316p8/1222367.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This is a tale that has been told countless times, but to make it stand out Cornwell had to make his retelling unique. In his version Lancelot is a fraud and Guinevere is quite possibly a complete whore who entrapped Arthur for power, not love; thus, this is far from the usual fairy tale. The characters are realistic and not without their flaws, so they are human and fallible. This is a far shot form the knights of the round table and the pure virtuous that Arthur represents. This made this novel not remotely predictable or a simple regurgitation of a tale we already know. <br><br>The movement of Christianity through the reduction of the druids is also apparent. It is intriguing to see the rivalries created by these religious differences. I like the way the Druid’s, though relying on and believing in magic, appear to have no magical abilities but are driven by what they perceive as their magic powers and knowledge. I think Cornwell has been very subtle here because without openly suggesting that their magic was impossible, he does show us that their magic is slowly fading. This gives the novel a more realistic setting, a more historical setting, rather than the usual fantastical nature of tales surrounding Arthur Pendragon. <br><br>I enjoyed this novel, but not as much as those in the Saxon Stories. Derfel lacks the charisma and will of Uhtred. And for that reason I gave this a four star rating rather than the five it could have earnt. <br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="17910171777263351609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7884929980620134492">This is my favorite Cornwell series (it's Cornwell's too), for it covers my favorite historical era--that mysterious gap in between the Roman departure and the Saxon Invasion. This retelling of Arthur works so well because it's divorced from Mallory. <br/><br/>I love BC's ability to pull me into the muddy, primitive Dark Age Britain world. He's one of the very best at avoiding anachronisms, a skill which gets so little praise. None of his characters feel like modernistic men and women dressed up in 5th Ce</span>
  <span id="freeText7884929980620134492" style="display:none">This is my favorite Cornwell series (it's Cornwell's too), for it covers my favorite historical era--that mysterious gap in between the Roman departure and the Saxon Invasion. This retelling of Arthur works so well because it's divorced from Mallory. <br /><br />I love BC's ability to pull me into the muddy, primitive Dark Age Britain world. He's one of the very best at avoiding anachronisms, a skill which gets so little praise. None of his characters feel like modernistic men and women dressed up in 5th Century garb. They fear the powers of magic, gods, and fate. The majority of the people are ignorant, living in dirty hovels with no boots. The world is vast and mysterious. <br /><br />The line-by-line writing is excellent, as are the story arcs of this tale. <br /><br />Highly suggest this series, as well as most of BC's work.<br /></span>

  <span id="freeText319833541691722196" style="display:none"><b>The horn sounded a third time, and suddenly I knew I would live, and I was weeping for joy and all our spearmen were half crying and half shouting and the earth was shuddering with the hooves of those Godlike men who were riding to our rescue.<br /><br />For Arthur, at last, had come.</b><br /><br /><br />Dun-da-da-da!<br /><br />Presenting a saga so epic it needs three pages to list the characters, two pages to mention the places and another two pages of maps! Here we have Arthur, the just and fair, brave Galahad and cowardly Lancelot, hiding behind his mother's skirts. All the old favorites get to mix it up with some new characters. Can you imagine a heaven filled with only the meek? <br />What a dreadful idea. The food would get cold while everyone passed the dishes to everyone else."</b><br /><br />Happily, there are two more books in the Arthur series.<br />Cornwell, I think this is the beginning of a beautiful friendship.</span>

<div id="review_2547024205" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2547024205" />
    <a title="Will.J.R. Gwynne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86504940-will-j-r-gwynne"><img alt="Will.J.R. Gwynne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540041401p2/86504940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2547024205?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will.J.R. Gwynne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will.J.R. Gwynne" href="/user/show/86504940-will-j-r-gwynne">Will.J.R. Gwynne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11365380-the-winter-king">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/86504940-will-j-r-gwynne?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/86504940-will-j-r-gwynne?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/86504940-will-j-r-gwynne?shelf=arthurian">arthurian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/86504940-will-j-r-gwynne?shelf=favourite-series">favourite-series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2547024205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2731285985265228033">My BookNest review - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>http://booknest.eu/component/k2/william/1590-the-winter-king-the-warlord-chronicles-1-by-bernard-cornwell-book-review</a><br/><br/><b>“The bards sing of love, they celebrate slaughter, they extol kings and flatter queens, but were I a poet I would write in praise of friendship.”</b><br/><br/>If you love stories consisting of memorable characters you love and despise, fantastic storytelling, stunning action sequences and moral lessons, then you will adore this historical telling of the chronicles of Arthur</span>
  <span id="freeText2731285985265228033" style="display:none">My BookNest review - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">http://booknest.eu/component/k2/william/1590-the-winter-king-the-warlord-chronicles-1-by-bernard-cornwell-book-review</a><br /><br /><b>“The bards sing of love, they celebrate slaughter, they extol kings and flatter queens, but were I a poet I would write in praise of friendship.”</b><br /><br />If you love stories consisting of memorable characters you love and despise, fantastic storytelling, stunning action sequences and moral lessons, then you will adore this historical telling of the chronicles of Arthur and the story of his life.<br /><br />The Winter King is the first book in The Warlord Chronicles which is a unique take on the story of Arthur during the dark ages. I have always loved the tales of Arthur and his warriors, and this is not an exception. It is the best book I have read which has the story of Arthur as its main concept.<br /><br /><b>“Fate is inexorable."</b><br /><br />Cornwell chooses certain aspects and adapts others from the common stories so the reader cannot predetermine the events which shall occur, and so the story which is told is a new one. He produces this book in a masterful style overflowing with immersive action and fantastic characters.<br /><br />The sole point of view is Derfel, was born a Saxon but raised a Briton in the kingdom of Dumnonia, which is a kingdom residing in southern Briton. He features as the main character in the Winter King. He is a man who values loyalty and kindness, and proves it many times. It is hard not to fall in love with him as the stories progress and you witness his faults and virtues.<br /><br />The prose of Bernard Cornwell is superb as the description and storytelling flows brilliantly. It is fluid and smooth making it an easy job to continue reading this large book as the plot line constantly develops in a manner which disallowed me from being bored at any point.<br /><br />The Winter King is a definite five star rating in my opinion due to one of my favourite writing styles I have encountered and the way it captured many of my favourite aspects in stories.<br /><br />Impending exams are absorbing most of my leisure time, but almost every moment of freedom I have gained in the last few days consisted of me reading this. It took a serious level of self-control to resist devouring this novel with a few long sittings.<br /><br />This is one of, if not my favourite historical fiction novel I have had the pleasure to read and I shall be instantly be borrowing my father’s copy of Enemy of God, which is the second book of this series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2731285985265228033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7728507019587252808">Why is this written in such a dry, boring narrative style? Bernard Cornwell's books can be exciting and full of period detail that really engage the reader. This one is sadly not like that. There is a helpful list of characters and places (and map) at the front of the book, for those of us unfamiliar with all the Welsh names. I dutifully consulted this every time a new name was mentioned but after 50 pages was just so very bored by it all. Where's the action? All reported. Where's the conversati</span>
  <span id="freeText7728507019587252808" style="display:none">Why is this written in such a dry, boring narrative style? Bernard Cornwell's books can be exciting and full of period detail that really engage the reader. This one is sadly not like that. There is a helpful list of characters and places (and map) at the front of the book, for those of us unfamiliar with all the Welsh names. I dutifully consulted this every time a new name was mentioned but after 50 pages was just so very bored by it all. Where's the action? All reported. Where's the conversation? Non-existent. The device of having a narrator, Derfel, just didn't work for me, although the events described sounded exciting enough. Please show, don't tell, Mr Cornwell. I had really been looking forward to reading this book.</span>

<div id="review_954277477" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/954277477" />
    <a title="Conor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30552336-conor"><img alt="Conor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1400278909p2/30552336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/954277477?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Conor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Conor" href="/user/show/30552336-conor">Conor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30552336-conor?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30552336-conor?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30552336-conor?shelf=awesome-books-are-awesome">awesome-books-are-awesome</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer954277477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3552489463343791588">4.5 stars <br/><br/>The legend of Arthur has been told time and again over the centuries. From ancient British folk tales to 5 season of 'Merlin', from 15th Century French verse to 'The Mists of Avalon'. With this book Cornwell has left his mark on that tradition. He's taken a tale examined from almost every angle and made it his own. Most of all he's written a story filled with complicated characters, visceral battles and ambitious intrigues in a brutal, immersive setting. <br/><br/>The protagonist of this story i</span>
  <span id="freeText3552489463343791588" style="display:none">4.5 stars <br /><br />The legend of Arthur has been told time and again over the centuries. From ancient British folk tales to 5 season of 'Merlin', from 15th Century French verse to 'The Mists of Avalon'. With this book Cornwell has left his mark on that tradition. He's taken a tale examined from almost every angle and made it his own. Most of all he's written a story filled with complicated characters, visceral battles and ambitious intrigues in a brutal, immersive setting. <br /><br />The protagonist of this story is Derfel Cadarn. A humble, straight-forward soldier Derfel is in a position to observe the complicated characters involved in the military and political struggles to decide the fate, and faith (lol @ puns) of Britain. Derfel also gives us an engaging insight into the terror and exhilaration of fighting in a shield wall. These sections, which bring to life the mud, blood, exhaustion, fear and pain of frontline combat are some of the most intense of the book. The framing device, with Derfal now an old monk telling his story, created some extremely poignant moments.<br /><br />Arthur is extremely well-written, although his character sometimes feels too noble, especially given how bleak the rest of this world is. To be fair, Arthurian legend usually holds Arthur up as the pinnacle of all that is right and in this series Cornwell portrays him as the one hope for goodness in a world filled with evil. For all of his saintliness (although saints in this book are often knobs Coughs* Sansum Coughs*) Arthur's vanity, ambition and occasional reckless idealism made him more than a 2-D representation of heroism and instead showed him to be a flawed, realistic character, making his struggles all the more powerful. <br /><br />In the early stages of this book I found Owain (the old king's champion) to be a brilliantly written character, a complex villain and a great source of conflict. On one hand Owain was a great warrior who was generous and kind to the men under his command (including Derfal) on the other hand he was openly corrupt, raped a captive woman (this was a disturbingly frequent occurrence in the story, at times the frequency of rape went beyond realistic in this setting and into being gratuitous) and mercilessly slaughtered a defenceless village to line his own pockets. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Due to his scummy actions I didn't find him an overly sympathetic character, as a result his death pretty early on didn't really affect me. However as the book progressed I felt his absence more keenly, especially as a bunch of much less compelling villains began to appear. I also thought that Derfal suddenly siding against him and his abrupt death cut out what could have been a really interesting contest for Derfal's loyalty between Owain's pragmatism (represented by his faith in the shield-wall) and Arthur's idealism (his love of heavy cavalry) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />In contrast Lancelot was a character with all the depth and complexity of a WWE villain (or 'heel' for those of you in the know...). Lancelot was shown to be vain, cowardly, arrogant, bullying and manipulative. Cornwell made a few half-hearted attempts at making him sympathetic and realistic but vague references to him only being a tool to Derfal because he fears him and his kindness to people who don't threaten him were never actually shown in the text and as a result don't have any real impact. In any situation he could be counted on to do the worst thing possible and this lack of complexity made the absence of Owain all the more obvious. <br /><br />The setting in this book, often stark and bleak, was brought to life brilliantly. The violence, turmoil and savagery of Dark Ages Britain were captured perfectly. Cornwell captured this sense of danger even better than Iggulden in 'Genghis'. The impact of religion was explored intelligently in this book as well. The conflict between druidism and Christianity and the effect it had on politics was well-handled. The presence of other cults and sects in the background served to give the portrayal of religion even more depth and to further ingrain it in the world. I did struggle with the use of magic in this book however. Magic, especially druids, play a major role in this story however the fact that all of this 'magic' is so obviously a sham made it's frequent, and supposedly threatening, use seem ridiculous. The behaviour of druids and other 'mage' types throughout the plot bordered on comical and the characters' naiveté to these obvious hoaxes was frustrating. <br /><br />Overall this story was a great example of historical fiction and a unique and powerful retelling of the Legend of Arthur. I'm definitely going to continue on with this series.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3552489463343791588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18810398-andy?shelf=top10-2018">top10-2018</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer998915897" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15807864729118258310">Not oft, actually hardly ever, do I read a second series by an author set in a diffo historical period for fear of more of the same but jus a diffo setting (its happened with others) but here I am giving Bernard Cornwell a go with his Arthurian saga – Its only a short series & having heard many good things, I’m intending to bang the lot of in one go (3 books) to complete my Summer read.<br/><br/>It’s also one of my favourite legends so I hope he does it justice!<br/><br/>The story begins with a scribe (Derfel) at a</span>
  <span id="freeText15807864729118258310" style="display:none">Not oft, actually hardly ever, do I read a second series by an author set in a diffo historical period for fear of more of the same but jus a diffo setting (its happened with others) but here I am giving Bernard Cornwell a go with his Arthurian saga – Its only a short series &amp; having heard many good things, I’m intending to bang the lot of in one go (3 books) to complete my Summer read.<br /><br />It’s also one of my favourite legends so I hope he does it justice!<br /><br />The story begins with a scribe (Derfel) at a desk who is writing the story of Arthur, the Warlord who should have been King so it goes........... The story proper starts conventionally with Uther Pendragon &amp; the birth of a son called.......? Derfel himself, Morgan, Mordred &amp; Merlin’s court (minus Merlin) at Glastonbury soon follow all being familiar names in the lore of Arthur. The lay of the land is set, illustrated by a map of the time C 480 AD in the land of the Britons. The story flows into birthrights, succession and politics across the kingdom &amp; the ensuing war with the interloping Saxons from the East. Real place names of the time are used which are on the map &amp; its authenticity is great to follow, making you feel part of the setting as you soon get used to them. It helps no doubt if you live in the region in question so it’s familiarity is already imbedded. <br /><br />I’m hooked from the start, enjoying the tale, the characters &amp; the period, which is portrayed in a feasible way (so much is still really unknown about post-Romano Britain). The characterisations of the players of Arthurian lore are spot-on for me &amp; make for an excellent read. The politics are involved with petty rivalries, jostling for power &amp; certain kings trying to be top dog of all the kingdoms of the Britons as all the while the Saxon threat becomes more prevalent as they encroach from the East. There is also a tie in with Brittany which is still part of the British sphere of influence. The battle scenes are expertly told &amp; easy to visualise, epic in their content.<br /><br />Will the Britons unite? Will the Saxons prevail? That is the crux of the story. Enjoy.<br /><br />A clear 5 stars for me as I can’t fault it...... plus Ive hardly given any 5’s this year! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15807864729118258310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2056571854" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer4646168632448014179">Firstly I'd like to thank Craig for an awesome first buddy read and for putting up with me in general. I look forward to reading with you again, if you'll have me. <br/><br/>The Winter King is a tremendously well written book. Cornwell is an amazing author and I can't wait to give some of his other books a go. Looking at you Last Kingdom!<br/><br/>This one gets a 3 star rating from me. I think 3 stars adequately represents my overall enjoyment of the book. I liked the story, loved the writing. But found it hard to </span>
  <span id="freeText4646168632448014179" style="display:none">Firstly I'd like to thank Craig for an awesome first buddy read and for putting up with me in general. I look forward to reading with you again, if you'll have me. <br /><br />The Winter King is a tremendously well written book. Cornwell is an amazing author and I can't wait to give some of his other books a go. Looking at you Last Kingdom!<br /><br />This one gets a 3 star rating from me. I think 3 stars adequately represents my overall enjoyment of the book. I liked the story, loved the writing. But found it hard to stay motivated and difficult to read at times. <br /><br />I found it hard to grasp who was who and the names of places and characters were difficult for me. I am not familiar with any Arthurian tales or retellings so this was all new territory for me. <br />Also, I do suffer with dyslexia and whilst for the most part I don't normally have too many issues, the spelling and letters did make this a challenging read for me. This I know is a personal gripe, but a gripe nonetheless. <br /><br />The writing was truly enjoyable and so many of the characters were multi layered and unique. I loved Arthur. There were also many different themes present in this book. Mainly themes of Love and honour often a combination of the two, and some of the speeches were very impressive. <br /><br />Overall I think part of my lack of interest came from the book hangover that Assassins Fate by Robin Hobb has left me in. But I also have a feeling that this wasn't quite my cup of tea. I can see why so many people think this is a 5 star read. <br />At this point I'm not going to continue with the trilogy as I feel I will probably have to force myself to do so. Reading should be a hobby, not a chore. So I'll try another Cornwell book instead. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4646168632448014179" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1360568010" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1360568010" />
    <a title="Tosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43670791-tosh"><img alt="Tosh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515175943p2/43670791.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1360568010?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tosh" href="/user/show/43670791-tosh">Tosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43670791-tosh?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43670791-tosh?shelf=bernard-cornwell">bernard-cornwell</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360568010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2696461415837188640"><i>
  <b>
    <blockquote>These are the tales of Arthur, the Warlord, the King that Never Was, the Enemy of God and…the best man I ever knew.</blockquote>
  </b>
</i><br/>What I loved about reading this tale for the first time is that I had no expectations. Of course, I did have a little knowledge of Arthur, but nothing that would give me an impression of who he <i>should</i> be. I knew he was a king and possibly a Christian (not in this version). And I had also heard of Galahad, Lancelot, Merlin and Camelot, but basically, this was all new to m</span>
  <span id="freeText2696461415837188640" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>
    <blockquote>These are the tales of Arthur, the Warlord, the King that Never Was, the Enemy of God and…the best man I ever knew.</blockquote>
  </b>
</i><br />What I loved about reading this tale for the first time is that I had no expectations. Of course, I did have a little knowledge of Arthur, but nothing that would give me an impression of who he <i>should</i> be. I knew he was a king and possibly a Christian (not in this version). And I had also heard of Galahad, Lancelot, Merlin and Camelot, but basically, this was all new to me.<br /><br />Derfel writes from a monastery at the request of Queen Ingrain who is infatuated with the exploits of Arthur, famed warrior and protector of Dumnonia. Derfel, once a sworn warrior and friend to Arthur, now remembers the days when Arthur sought peace and justice, but writes not only of his heroism, but of his most vulnerable moments. The tale begins with the birth of a new heir, a leaderless land, and desperate need for someone who can bring peace and safety until he is of age. Arthur will not be the <i>first</i> choice.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>Our task is to give Mordred a rich, peaceful kingdom, and to do that we have to make it a good and just kingdom. </blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br /><b>Arthur, Arthur, Arthur...</b> A man of honesty, kindness, passion, ambition, and such high expectations for himself. In his attempt to seek peace, he also made quite a few enemies. My gosh! <b>I loved him and wanted to throttle him at the same time.</b> Love does strange things to people, but he risked everything for Guinevere, and I can’t help but think what a shallow and conceited woman she is. Their union started out on the wrong foot, and I have my suspicions that things will not go well in the future. She’s a sly one. As for Arthur, he’s blinded by his love and his need to be loved, but not just by Guinevere. As much as he wants peace and justice, he stumbles over his own ambitions, maybe because he refuses to see the falsity in others. The mercy he extends, and the trust he gives may come back to bite him later. <i>I foresee tragedy in his future.</i><br /><br />Lancelot is a turd! (<i>’turd’ being from the Cornwell dictionary</i>) I’m not sure what his place is in the original legends but he is, after Sansum, my least favorite character in this book. He’s a lying, sniveling momma’s boy, and I hope he’s exposed for the worthless prince he is. <br /><br />Sansum. I already reserved <i>turd</i> for Lancelot, but he’s lower than low. No surprise to find a phony priest hording gold, being a cruel, bitter man and taking advantage of his novices. I wish he weren’t in the story, because he disgusts me. As a Christian, these characters give me the most frustration because I know there are, have been and will always be people like him. <br /><br />Galahad on the other hand, was such a loyal warrior and friend. We don’t get to see a whole lot of his character, but I’m hoping he will have a more central place in the series eventually.<br /><br /><b>Merlin!</b> I love Merlin. He doesn’t show up for a long while. I was beginning to wonder if he ever would. If he weren’t such a mean old man, he reminded me a little of Gandalf, always away on some journey searching for more information, returning when you least expect, only to leave just as abruptly. I love his little gibes, his indifference to the events outside his own ambition, but that he meddles anyway. The fact that most of his power, and that of the other Druids, is all based on superstition and fear, and not actually magic is a nice change of pace. He walks about as confidently as if he could actually cast an enchantment or perform a miracle. It’s funny to think he holds no actual power and yet can move kings and armies at his word. <br /><br /><b>I’ve missed out on a wonder legend for far too long</b>, but if it had to be any book to introduce me then I’m happy it was this one, because I just love Bernard Cornwell’s writing: his gritty details, his ability to make you feel what his characters feel and see what they see, and the fact that no matter how much I want to turn away, I can’t. Even when the characters are rotten or the details too much there’s nothing like a well-written story that makes you feel the anguish, the cruelty, the depravity, the love, the loyalty, and the ambition of those characters. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2696461415837188640" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3276662027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3276662027" />
    <a title="ChopinFC" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51633786-chopinfc"><img alt="ChopinFC" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545283749p2/51633786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3276662027?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ChopinFC" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ChopinFC" href="/user/show/51633786-chopinfc">ChopinFC</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51633786-chopinfc?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51633786-chopinfc?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51633786-chopinfc?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51633786-chopinfc?shelf=series">series</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3276662027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1013098045787513882"><b>5 Stars (Amazing)<br/><br/><br/>What can be said about the perfect historical fiction book about one of Brittain's most beloved heroic character?</b> <br/><br/> <i>The Winter King: A Novel of Arthur</i> was a brilliant first series into the world of warlord Arthur. I learned about Cornwell's masterpiece from fellow GR friends, and frankly I've never read any previous Arthur legends- as I was never that thrilled into a character piece about Brittain. Yet, something about the vivaciousness and excitement of the reviews about this se</span>
  <span id="freeText1013098045787513882" style="display:none"><b>5 Stars (Amazing)<br><br><br>What can be said about the perfect historical fiction book about one of Brittain's most beloved heroic character?</b> <br><br> <i>The Winter King: A Novel of Arthur</i> was a brilliant first series into the world of warlord Arthur. I learned about Cornwell's masterpiece from fellow GR friends, and frankly I've never read any previous Arthur legends- as I was never that thrilled into a character piece about Brittain. Yet, something about the vivaciousness and excitement of the reviews about this series made me fall head in, and I've never looked back!<br><br><u>Let's start with the basics: <i>The Winter King: A Novel of Arthur</i> is unbelievably well written story, full of intrigue, action, memorable characters and dynamic characters! </u> Another caveat that is important to consider-<b> although this book retells an original 'version' of Arthur's legend and ascent to power- it mostly focuses on amazing and unforgettable characters around the epoch!! </b> <br><br><i>The Winter King: A Novel of Arthur is as much about Arthur as it is about the incredibly powerful wizard 'Merlin', Uther the high-king, the beautiful Kywin, Merlin's 'maid-wizards', the Brittons, the Saxons and the ever raging wars.</i> <b>Yet, the pivotal and most dynamic character comes in the likes of Arthur's best friend, Derfel Cadarn, and the narrator, now an old monk who recounts his journey with Arthur and the ever lasting wars!</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1587487141i/29332686._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="290" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Derfel Cardan</i><br><br><br>Despite being published 23 years ago, <i>The Winter King</i> feels absolutely modern and fresh in both style and execution. Much of that is owed to Cornwell's prowess with words and storytelling. <b>Cornwell is a master storyteller, that I'm convinced! </b> The books reminds me so much of great tales like LOTR, or even GOT. The retrospective narrative from Derfel's perspective adds much value to the story, specially as he is Arthur's best friend and most competent warrior. The battle scenes are incredible, specially in the last 25% of the book, and another favorite author of mine, 'John Gwyne', has admitted using Cornwell as a big influence in his masterful 'The Faitfhull and Fallen' series. The 'battle soldier wall' used in 'Faithful' is a direct homage to Cornwell's incredible battle scenes, where the 'wall' is originally used. <br><br><br><b>Those of you wanting an amazing story, with legendary characters, incredible battle scenes and a maddened wizard, will find bliss in the masterful <i>The Winter King</i></b></span>
  <a data-text-id="1013098045787513882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16915331932864476974">A different take on the tales of Camelot and Arthur. Book one deals with the early struggles to start building the kingdom and bringing the Britons together. I enjoyed this darker version which made me think Lancelot or some relative of his must have pissed in one of Cornwell's relative's Cheerios once upon a time. He and Guinevere definitely take a battering in this version. This version makes more sense in many ways than the more romanticized version of the tale. <br/><br/>Galahad on the other hand come</span>
  <span id="freeText16915331932864476974" style="display:none">A different take on the tales of Camelot and Arthur. Book one deals with the early struggles to start building the kingdom and bringing the Britons together. I enjoyed this darker version which made me think Lancelot or some relative of his must have pissed in one of Cornwell's relative's Cheerios once upon a time. He and Guinevere definitely take a battering in this version. This version makes more sense in many ways than the more romanticized version of the tale. <br /><br />Galahad on the other hand comes out shining and his connection and relationship to Lancelot was very interesting. <br /><br />I was wanting more Merlin during the entirety of the book. Who wouldn't want more Merlin? His appearances are generally brief and somewhat anticlimactic. <br /><br />My one major issue with the book was that it was written from a past perspective from one of the characters. This isn't uncommon for Cornwell as Uhtred in Saxon Stories does the same. I think the major difference is Uhtred's voice is one of passion, motivation and scorn. While Derfal's is one of a historical scholar who is reviewing with dispassionate fact finding even though he's reviewing from his own memories. I'm not saying that was the case for the entirety of the book for major sections it was. <br /><br />Still I look forward to the rest of the series. </span>
