<div id="review_94742121" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/94742121" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/545336-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329191662p2/545336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/94742121?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/545336-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312144470" href="/book/show/120287.The_Winter_King">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=xcharity-2012">xcharity-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/545336-mike?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer94742121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17944911055295321209">TWK <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68520.The_Winter_King__The_Arthur_Books___1_' title='The Winter King (The Arthur Books, #1) by Bernard Cornwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/books/1311987677s/68520.jpg' alt='The Winter King (The Arthur Books, #1) by Bernard Cornwell' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a> is easy 5 stars for bringing this oft-told tale to us in a completely new (and I do mean new) way. <b>WARNING! Do Not read this book if you like your Arthur legend gauzy, frilly, magicky and cheesy.</b> All previous Arthurian tales pale in comparison to Mr. Cornwell’s version. All of the usual suspects are present here but you will not see many of them portrayed in the standard ways. You will also meet a host of new companions and enemies. It is safe to say that you will never look at Lancelot</span>
  <span id="freeText17944911055295321209" style="display:none">TWK <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68520.The_Winter_King__The_Arthur_Books___1_" title="The Winter King (The Arthur Books, #1) by Bernard Cornwell" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/books/1311987677s/68520.jpg" alt="The Winter King (The Arthur Books, #1) by Bernard Cornwell" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> is easy 5 stars for bringing this oft-told tale to us in a completely new (and I do mean new) way. <b>WARNING! Do Not read this book if you like your Arthur legend gauzy, frilly, magicky and cheesy.</b> All previous Arthurian tales pale in comparison to Mr. Cornwell’s version. All of the usual suspects are present here but you will not see many of them portrayed in the standard ways. You will also meet a host of new companions and enemies. It is safe to say that you will never look at Lancelot, Guinevere and Morgan in the same light. Arthur is completely believable as a real person. While he is noble and also fallible, this Arthur is an implacable and ruthless warrior when needed. You will care for the new characters in Arthur’s service and despise his fiendish enemies. Merlin is a real treat, never easily categorized. Mordred is despicable in a way that makes sense.<br><br>This first book in the trilogy introduces Arthur as he tries to stop the Britons fighting each other and unite them to oppose the invading Saxons. Some of the venues are familiar and some are not. You will meet and follow Derfel, who tells the tale long after it occurred, and he is a central and appealing character.<br><br>What sets this story apart is Cornwell’s attempt to show a realistic portrayal of what a Welsh Arthur would have been like. He also tries to show what life was like at the time. (If you need vowels to pronounce names and words, you will be struggling. His characters often have names like the trolls in Tolkien’s tales and the place names look like sites in Mordor.) While Cornwell states up front this is not his attempt to write a true history, it becomes so realistic you will think it must have been so.<br><br>The Winter King is set in a cold, dreary time when the Britons are fighting each other while the Saxons slowly invade the eastern part of the island. There is a recognition that the glories of Roman civilization, learning and progress are fading and that the Britons cannot build on that legacy. A long dark period is looming. Adding to this mix is a war between Christianity and paganism. It isn’t pretty but Cornwell’s story is so rich and pungent, I found myself caring for these characters like few others I have ever read. You will too.<br></span>
  <a data-text-id="17944911055295321209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_258670094" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/258670094" />
    <a title="Magdalena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7356331-magdalena"><img alt="Magdalena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454602635p2/7356331.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/258670094?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Magdalena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Magdalena" href="/user/show/7356331-magdalena">Magdalena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7297518-the-winter-king">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7356331-magdalena?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7356331-magdalena?shelf=myths-legends">myths-legends</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer258670094&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer258670094" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5674130273077999313">So everyone knows about the Arthurian legends and that there isn't much historical evidence (if any) that Arthur existed. That said, this book strikes me as an accurate account of what it would have been like if such a warlord lived. The historical setting- Saxon raids, Irish conquerors, religious disputes, traces of Roman civilization, all that good stuff, is pretty accurate. Bernard Cornwell evidently did serious research into the way of life in 500ad, and it all gives a very authentic vibe. T</span>
  <span id="freeText5674130273077999313" style="display:none">So everyone knows about the Arthurian legends and that there isn't much historical evidence (if any) that Arthur existed. That said, this book strikes me as an accurate account of what it would have been like if such a warlord lived. The historical setting- Saxon raids, Irish conquerors, religious disputes, traces of Roman civilization, all that good stuff, is pretty accurate. Bernard Cornwell evidently did serious research into the way of life in 500ad, and it all gives a very authentic vibe. Things are portrayed as they were, with warriors wearing light armor to be more agile, Saxons wearing simple and crude armor (I absolutely praise the lack of impractical yet fancy demon-horned helmets), druids spiking their hair with dung, the lack of actual magic, and much more humanized characters.<br /><br />The narrator, Derfel Cadarn, is old and feeble, retelling Arthur's story to the current young queen Igraine. At this point (Book #1), some parts of the story are compromised due to his own beliefs (as pointed out by Igraine). Chiefly, his hatred towards Lancelot and the accusations of cowardice, and the praise to the Star of Powys and her beauty. We can't know if his descriptions are somewhat biased in some aspects, but that makes for an entertaining story. (On a lesser note, I also quite enjoyed Igraine's impatience to know what happens in the end, for I felt the same way. Derfel, however, kept the story linear, without jumping to spoilers, much to her discontent.)<br /><br />The main character, Arthur, is a very decent fellow. He empathizes with everyone (and by everyone I do mean everyone. Except Saxons.) and tries to be fair in judgement, tactful, respectful, brave, and honorable. Perhaps his major flaws are his love of Guinevere, his stubbornness, and his ambition. His ultimate goal is to unite Britain against foreign invasions and to secure his protegee the throne of Dumnonia, while leading that peace himself. Despite all this, he insults Powys by first betrothing the powisian princess and then abruptly marrying Guinevere out of mad, passionate, foolish love. (Lust?) And so the ultimate war begins, facing enemies on all sides- Franks fighting Armorica on the south (a kingdom Arthur had sworn to protect), Irish Blackshields and Kernow on the west, Siluria and Powys on the north, and Saxons on the East. War and madness all over the place, how much more real can this time period get? <br /><br />In the legends Merlin is a majestic, wise, kind even, Druid. In the book I thought he was more of a scumbag, using everyone for his own amusement, not caring for peoples' suffering. But yes, he was wise and feared by all, with the impatience at people's stupidity that I guess could only come with the knowledge and power he possessed. All in all, I liked him.<br /><br />As for a minor pet peeve, there is a ton of spit in this book. Everyone spits at everyone to insult them, to ward off evil, to release some anger, to prove a point, to curse... You get the idea.<br /><br />In conclusion, excellent, excellent book. Had me surprised at reading so much so suddenly without even realizing how much I had read. Recommended to people who want a realistic legend, who enjoy the Dark Ages, who like accurate historical settings, who believe chivalry shouldn't be dead, and who like ancient battles. I for one, am absolutely thrilled to read the next book in this series :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="5674130273077999313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_480818034" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/480818034" />
    <a title="Nate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6803829-nate"><img alt="Nate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413944642p2/6803829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/480818034?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate" href="/user/show/6803829-nate">Nate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=middle-ages">middle-ages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6803829-nate?shelf=creeps-perverts-psychos-killers">creeps-perverts-psychos-killers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer480818034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13203872494148527554">I haven't read all of Cornwell's books but from what I've read this is my absolute favorite so far. It's a fantastic vision of what Arthur's life might have really been like. The typical idea that seems to stick in people's heads is of this romantic story with gallant knights, fair maidens and all that boring stuff but when you think about it, there really was no room in the post-Roman Dark Ages Britain for that kind of shit. Don't get me wrong, there's plenty of heroism in this book but it's mo</span>
  <span id="freeText13203872494148527554" style="display:none">I haven't read all of Cornwell's books but from what I've read this is my absolute favorite so far. It's a fantastic vision of what Arthur's life might have really been like. The typical idea that seems to stick in people's heads is of this romantic story with gallant knights, fair maidens and all that boring stuff but when you think about it, there really was no room in the post-Roman Dark Ages Britain for that kind of shit. Don't get me wrong, there's plenty of heroism in this book but it's more the exception than the rule (and that's what made it so stirring for me). People are just fucking mean, cowardly, deceitful and sadistic in this book but that's what makes Arthur shine so brilliantly. On the other side of that coin, however, Cornwell writes him as a real person with flaws and failures. This depth extends to most of the rest of the characters.<br /><br />One of the things I really liked about the Saxon books is the sketchy, creepy magic-related stuff (I'm thinking of scenes particularly in, uh...I think <i>Lords of the North</i> and <i>Death of Kings</i>) and this is even more brilliantly realized in this book. As you'd expect, the "magic" in these books always turns out to be elaborate human trickery but the characters believe in it so fervently that it creates this spooky atmosphere and you really can see how real it was to these people. The Isle of the Dead, the Druids, all of the weird pagan rituals...good stuff.<br /><br />This book was just so full of memorable scenes, characters, locations...it really seems like Cornwell was really inspired when he wrote this one. I have extremely high hopes for the following two books of the trilogy. Even though I know what has to eventually happen it's still gripping reading for me. At this point, this seems like it's probably Cornwell's masterpiece. It goes above and beyond being "just" an adventure story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13203872494148527554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1499853249" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15064164533197746315">I have read many King Arthur novels and together with the High Queen series by Nancy McKenzie this is the one I liked best. It is also my favourite Bernard Cornwell novel. Set in the sixth century, after the Romans have left Britain Celtic Britain is threatened by Irish tribes from the West and Saxons from the East, as well as civil strife. Seen from the yes of the warrior Derfel, who serves Arthur. Arthur is regent to the crippled boy king, his nephew Mordred. Rich in cast of characters and fas</span>
  <span id="freeText15064164533197746315" style="display:none">I have read many King Arthur novels and together with the High Queen series by Nancy McKenzie this is the one I liked best. It is also my favourite Bernard Cornwell novel. Set in the sixth century, after the Romans have left Britain Celtic Britain is threatened by Irish tribes from the West and Saxons from the East, as well as civil strife. Seen from the yes of the warrior Derfel, who serves Arthur. Arthur is regent to the crippled boy king, his nephew Mordred. Rich in cast of characters and fascinating to read of all the different places in Britain at the time. Strong in battle scenes and politics but also in characterization and relationships and not without well developed women characters, Cornwell never disappoints and this will be a treat for anyone who loves Arthurian legend and Dark Ages history. Unusual but interesting characterizations of Merlin, Nimue, Guinevre and Morgan. Morgan is a priestess who has been hideously disfigured by fire and wear a gold mask. Merlin is a cynical enigma with the single minded determination to return Britain to the old ways and old gods. Guinevere is portrayed as petty and spiteful and Nimue as a beguiling priestess, determined to avenge her rape and mutilation by the evil King Gundleus of Siluria. Never a dull page. Really recreates both fantasy and history. climaxes with a victory in battle by King Arthur of against the forces of the kings of Powys and Siluria.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15064164533197746315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer113880236" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9713134029972899194">Excellent novel. Cornwell has truly taken the Arthurian legend and made it his own. All the old names are here, but often in much different forms than expected.<br/><br/>This tale is told by one of Arthur's warriors, Derfel. In his old age, Derfel has become a monk. While serving as such, he is writing the story of Arthur, who he knew personally.<br/><br/>Cornwell's Arthur is a delight. While maintaining a degree of the larger-than-life qualities we come to expect of this character, Cornwell makes him human. Gives </span>
  <span id="freeText9713134029972899194" style="display:none">Excellent novel. Cornwell has truly taken the Arthurian legend and made it his own. All the old names are here, but often in much different forms than expected.<br /><br />This tale is told by one of Arthur's warriors, Derfel. In his old age, Derfel has become a monk. While serving as such, he is writing the story of Arthur, who he knew personally.<br /><br />Cornwell's Arthur is a delight. While maintaining a degree of the larger-than-life qualities we come to expect of this character, Cornwell makes him human. Gives him flaws. While he strives to realize his dream of a free, united Britain, his shortcomings often get in the way. Derfel sees his flaws and his loyalty does not waver. <br /><br />This is probably the most "real" I've seen an Arthurian story done, due to the way Cornwell handles his characters. Some are heroic, some are villainous. Some go both ways. But they all have a human element that gives them authenticity. <br /><br />There are two more books in the series, and though this one left off at a good pause point, I'll certainly be seeking those out.</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of Arthur. </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29882975-manisha?shelf=series-trilogy">series-trilogy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29882975-manisha?shelf=genre-historical-fiction">genre-historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2167190437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16026565536257369198"><b>Actual review:</b> 4.5<br/><br/><i>“But fate, as Merlin always taught us, is inexorable. Life is a jest of the Gods, Merlin liked to claim, and there is no justice. You must learn to laugh, he once told me, or else you'll just weep yourself to death.”</i><br/><br/>This was absolutely fantastic! <br/><br/>We meet the same characters we’ve known through countless stories, re-imaginings and movies, and we see them in a world based in reality. The concept is one that many have attempted but none have pulled off satisfactorily…. until now.<br/><br/>T</span>
  <span id="freeText16026565536257369198" style="display:none"><b>Actual review:</b> 4.5<br /><br /><i>“But fate, as Merlin always taught us, is inexorable. Life is a jest of the Gods, Merlin liked to claim, and there is no justice. You must learn to laugh, he once told me, or else you'll just weep yourself to death.”</i><br /><br />This was absolutely fantastic! <br /><br />We meet the same characters we’ve known through countless stories, re-imaginings and movies, and we see them in a world based in reality. The concept is one that many have attempted but none have pulled off satisfactorily…. until now.<br /><br />The Winter King by Bernard Cornwell is not a story about Arthur Pendragon who pulls a sword from a stone and becomes the perfect ruler with his sidekick Merlin. The story is about Arthur, a general who tries to unite England during the Dark Ages. The story feels historic, realistic, gritty and brilliant. <br /><br />I admit that I went into this book knowing nothing (it’s my chosen method of reading), so I was quite surprised by the level of importance religion played in this story. The discussion of Christianity feels like the most important fact to take away from this novel. So, if the discussion of religion bothers you, I would suggest you give this book a miss. As a result, even though this is a 'fantasy' series, it feels more like historical fiction. And the writing is impeccable and easy to follow. <br /><br />A quick warning to those who are about to delve into this world:<br /><br />Beware the first half of the book. The set up of the world seems a bit forced and it moves slowly compared to the rest of the book. If it wasn’t for the fact that it took me quite a while to get into the book, I would have given it five stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16026565536257369198" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_55204461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/55204461" />
    <a title="Kate Quinn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2232084-kate-quinn"><img alt="Kate Quinn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534961434p2/2232084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/55204461?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate Quinn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate Quinn" href="/user/show/2232084-kate-quinn">Kate Quinn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer55204461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17951936966920823931">Hands down the best King Arthur fiction ever written. "The Winter King" begins Bernard Cornwell's trilogy of Arthur, the king who may have lived in early Britain, driven back the invading Saxons, and inspired a legend. Interestingly enough, Arthur is not the central hero of the book - that role falls to Derfel, a Saxon boy who escapes a Druid death pit to be raised by Britons and eventually become Arthur's right-hand man. Derfel is a very old man when the story begins, a monk in a Christian mona</span>
  <span id="freeText17951936966920823931" style="display:none">Hands down the best King Arthur fiction ever written. "The Winter King" begins Bernard Cornwell's trilogy of Arthur, the king who may have lived in early Britain, driven back the invading Saxons, and inspired a legend. Interestingly enough, Arthur is not the central hero of the book - that role falls to Derfel, a Saxon boy who escapes a Druid death pit to be raised by Britons and eventually become Arthur's right-hand man. Derfel is a very old man when the story begins, a monk in a Christian monastery who is writing the story of the leader he followed all his life. We meet Derfel as a boy, dreaming of becoming a warrior someday and in love with his Druid-in-training friend Nimue. The land is ruled by a baby king, Mordred, and is soon torn apart by war. Arthur rides to the rescue (Mordred is his nephew rather than his son; expect deviations from the normal Arthur myths) and Derfel wants nothing more than to serve him. Derfel will grow up, become a warrior, fall in love a few times, and eventually stand at Arthur's side in a cataclysmic war that begins when Arthur spurns a princess to marry a penniless beauty named Guinevere. "The Winter King" starts rather slowly - Cornwell admits he got too interested in laying out the scene than proceeding with the story - but stick with it. This is some of the best historical fiction ever written. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17951936966920823931" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7179017220066213353">Simply one of the best historical fiction reads out there. A unique retelling of the Arthur legend as it may have really happened. Arthur is a Celtic warlord in a post-roman Britain. Merlin is a druid, but his magic is more of he slight of hand variety. The story takes place against a ripe backdrop of change - Romano-Celts versus invading Saxons and Angles and the new Christian God against the Old Gods. Great stuff. Highly recommended.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1144378173" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1144378173" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8386404-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351707681p2/8386404.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1144378173?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/8386404-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7297518-the-winter-king">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8386404-richard?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1144378173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18343964657431174399">7.5/10<br/><br/>It took a while for me to get into this, I was overwhelmed with the amount of names thrust at me from the off. From the start you've learnt more characters names in the first few pages than most series will have in their entire run only for the majority of these to be killed off before you've remembered who they are! Luckily, things steadied after a while and you had a core group and things became more focused and the story was able to really progress.<br/><br/>I guess the story of King Arthur and M</span>
  <span id="freeText18343964657431174399" style="display:none">7.5/10<br /><br />It took a while for me to get into this, I was overwhelmed with the amount of names thrust at me from the off. From the start you've learnt more characters names in the first few pages than most series will have in their entire run only for the majority of these to be killed off before you've remembered who they are! Luckily, things steadied after a while and you had a core group and things became more focused and the story was able to really progress.<br /><br />I guess the story of King Arthur and Merlin and his knights is quite well know but the more I read the more I realised I knew very little actually other than the sword and a few of the knights names which made for a more entertaining read as I had no idea who would do what or who would meet a grisly doom.<br /><br />Cornwell has a tendency to stick in massive descriptive passages which I personally struggled with at times. However, even with that said, I noticed at the latter stages of the book I found myself thinking about the book when I wasn't reading it and I needed to know what happened next which is always a good sign. At the finish I was grateful that there are two more books in the series and hoping now all the main characters have been introduced the next book will get straight into things without introducing a list of people as long as the telephone book.<br /><br />If you like this try: "Azincourt" by Bernard Cornwell </span>
  <a data-text-id="18343964657431174399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_514153418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/514153418" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1662632-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540855273p2/1662632.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/514153418?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/1662632-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312144470" href="/book/show/120287.The_Winter_King">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1662632-richard?shelf=series">series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer514153418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18373101419880475887">This is the first of a trilogy in a magnificent telling of the Arthurian legend. Yeah, that one: Arthur, Merlin, Uther Pendragon, Guinevere, Morgan, Mordred, Lancelot, Galahad — the whole cast, as far as I know (well, Morgause appears to be missing).<br/><br/>I haven’t read any of the others Arthurian books, so I can’t comment on any linkages between this and the agglomeration of other tellings. My knowledge of this history/legend/mythos comes mostly from what I’ve picked up here and there, with a heavy d</span>
  <span id="freeText18373101419880475887" style="display:none">This is the first of a trilogy in a magnificent telling of the Arthurian legend. Yeah, that one: Arthur, Merlin, Uther Pendragon, Guinevere, Morgan, Mordred, Lancelot, Galahad — the whole cast, as far as I know (well, Morgause appears to be missing).<br /><br />I haven’t read any of the others Arthurian books, so I can’t comment on any linkages between this and the agglomeration of other tellings. My knowledge of this history/legend/mythos comes mostly from what I’ve picked up here and there, with a heavy dose of Monty Python, Camelot (the musical) and fond-if-vague recollections of the dark 1981 movie, <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0082348/" rel="nofollow noopener">Excalibur</a>.<br /><br />Bernard Cornwell relies on the point that what is “known” about Arthur is microscopic compared to the stories that have been told, and that gives him the leeway to tweak the legend in ways that aren’t available to most “historical” fiction. In other words, there is so much fiction already in the mixture that he can transpose the historical portion to where he thinks he will have the most fun. In his afterword, he admits that his choices are “capricious at best and fortified by the certainty that no real answer exists”. In the end, he locates his story in the part of Britain he loves best.<br /><br />The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/King_arthur" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia page</a> on King Arthur tells us he was <i>possibly</i> a real human being around the early sixth century. This is long after the first Christian missionaries came to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religion_in_Wales#History" rel="nofollow noopener">Wales</a> and maybe after <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Druidism#Possible_late_survival_of_Insular_druidism" rel="nofollow noopener">druidism was largely eliminated</a>. But in Cornwell’s tale, druids play an important role, which distinguishes this from the heavily Christianized tales that dominate the most popular tellings of Arthur (e.g., the Holy Grail — although the Grail does make an appearance, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thirteen_Treasures_of_the_Island_of_Britain#The_Cauldron_of_Dyrnwch_the_Giant" rel="nofollow noopener">sort of</a>).<br /><br />Many others parts of the story are also shifted a bit: the major characters don’t have the same relationships to one another — for example, Galahad is Lancelot’s illegitimate half-brother, not his illegitimate son. Mordred isn’t Arthur’s son, but nephew (and an infant in this first volume).<br /><br />The very first portion — fifty, maybe sixty pages? — is a little slow. Derfel Cadarn, the narrator is an old monk, writing this history. (He is based on the historical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saint_Derfel" rel="nofollow noopener">Saint Derfel Gadarn</a>; the book is written in the first person from his perspective). As his tale begins, he is a young man of Saxon ancestry who had been adopted as an orphan by Merlin. Merlin is absent, and no one knows where he is, when he might return, or even if he is alive. The High King, Uther Pendragon, has sent his heir and grandson, Mordred, to Merlin’s camp at Ynys Wydryn.<br /><br />The barrage of Welsh (or Cornish, I’m not completely sure) words and names can be pretty overwhelming, and several times I was tempted to abandon the book and move on to something else. But eventually the situation becomes clear, and some action kicks in, and the for the rest of the book there is no question that we’re in the hands of a master storyteller.<br /><br />As the chapters go by, more of the expected Arthurian characters are added to the mix. Cornwell does an excellent job at making Arthur a complex character (Merlin remains mostly an enigma), torn between his belief that he can forge Britain into a unified people, able to resist the despised Saxons, and his passions; chief among them, the <i>femme fatale</i>, Guinevere. The politics certainly seems accurate. Perhaps the real draw is the military aspect: Cornwell gives us a real feel for what spear-and-shield fighting was about. Maintaining the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shield_wall" rel="nofollow noopener">shield wall</a> was paramount; as long as it stood your side would probably get by with few casualties, but woe to those facing the melee after it collapsed, especially if they had been flanked.<br /><br />I suspect someone could spend many hours tracking Cornwell’s usage of actual historical names and places. For example, the final battle in this book is at “Lugg Vale”, which was actually the site of a very similar fight in the early fifteenth century, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Bryn_Glas" rel="nofollow noopener">Battle of Bryn Glas</a>. Thus, the “historical” half of “historical fiction” is well rooted.<br /><br />Since I’ve only read this first book in the trilogy, I can’t endorse the whole series, although I certainly plan on continuing into the next volume, and I can recommend <i>
  <b>The Winter King</b>
</i> to anyone who enjoys a rousing good story.<br />­</span>
  <a data-text-id="18373101419880475887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2851165327" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2851165327" />
    <a title="Sud666" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666"><img alt="Sud666" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418437112p2/36890366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2851165327?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sud666" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sud666" href="/user/show/36890366-sud666">Sud666</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36890366-sud666?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2851165327" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7793765744465472216">Bernard Cornwell is one of my favorite writers of historical fiction. Thus, I was surprised to see him write a book about Arthur. I am certainly glad that I picked this up. It is a very strange book in that it technically belongs on my "fantasy" shelf, but the accuracy of the settings and weapons makes it a weak "historical fiction" as well. Mr. Cornwell admits as much, in the notes, when he points out that the monk Gildas's "De Excidio et Conquestu Britanniae" (written during the 540s) about th</span>
  <span id="freeText7793765744465472216" style="display:none">Bernard Cornwell is one of my favorite writers of historical fiction. Thus, I was surprised to see him write a book about Arthur. I am certainly glad that I picked this up. It is a very strange book in that it technically belongs on my "fantasy" shelf, but the accuracy of the settings and weapons makes it a weak "historical fiction" as well. Mr. Cornwell admits as much, in the notes, when he points out that the monk Gildas's "De Excidio et Conquestu Britanniae" (written during the 540s) about the Britons defeating a Saxon invasion never mentions Arthur. It is likely, as most historians believe, that he was a warlord though not a king.<br /><br />That is the premise for this story. Uther Pendragon is old, his bastard Arthur is banished to Britanny to learn from King Ban of Benoic and Merlin has been missing for several years. Modred, Uther's crippled heir, is born and Arthur returns to pledge himself as his protector. Meanwhile, a series of gaffes (this Arthur is noble but dense) causes the Britons to fight each other as the Saxons encroach on more land.<br /><br />It is in this setting that we see Arthur. The story is told through the eyes of Derfel, one of Arthur's warriors, and it has many of the characters from the better-known versions of the Arthurian legend. But there are subtle differences and I think, in order to make it more 'realistic", the changes work. Even the powers of Nimue or Merlin are no more, nor less, efficacious due to the sheer credulity of the people. Curses, wizards, druids, etc all had a deep-seated psychological appeal to people from this time and Cornwell shows their influence (though never ascribing them supernatural powers).<br /><br />I really enjoyed this "realistic" retelling of the Arthurian legend and anyone who enjoys either historical fiction or fantasy will appreciate this story. Apparently, this is only the first book so count me in for the rest of this series. Cornwell really does a great job with the legend and is able to retell it in a very realistic setting.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7793765744465472216" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

One of my favorite series is his medieval Grail Quest series, which follows an English archer during The Hundred Years War. It’s fantastic. I love it (and highly recommend it). Surely, then, I would love <b>The Winter King</b> – <i>Arthurian legend by Cornwell, for crying out loud!</i> Well, read on and find out…<br/><br/><a href='https://jpashman.com/2018/05/24/casual-book-review-the-winter-king-by-bernard-cornwell/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Read the rest of my review here.</a></span>

<div id="review_1877605179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1877605179" />
    <a title="Tammy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37399719-tammy"><img alt="Tammy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515462482p2/37399719.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1877605179?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tammy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tammy" href="/user/show/37399719-tammy">Tammy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37399719-tammy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1877605179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13911714721762955600">'The Bards sing of love, they celebrate slaughter, they extol kings and flatter queens, but were I a poet I would write in praise of friendship.'<br/><br/>This was an adventure. This was unique. Can't lay a finger on it but I know i've never read anything quite like this. I remember getting jarred at the start because of this, fearing this may not be my cup of tea after all, but even while contemplating whether to continue or not I knew deep down I was already hooked. I got used to the narrative, develope</span>
  <span id="freeText13911714721762955600" style="display:none">'The Bards sing of love, they celebrate slaughter, they extol kings and flatter queens, but were I a poet I would write in praise of friendship.'<br /><br />This was an adventure. This was unique. Can't lay a finger on it but I know i've never read anything quite like this. I remember getting jarred at the start because of this, fearing this may not be my cup of tea after all, but even while contemplating whether to continue or not I knew deep down I was already hooked. I got used to the narrative, developed a strong attachment to the characters, villains and all, and had a ton of fun reading Derfel's tale.<br /><br />Now I've read a lot of grimdark books, I thought I had seen it all but Cornwell laughed and said "Come child, behold: The Isle of the Dead". Even for an old timer like me this was unrelentingly gritty. But these were dark days, when the threat of war forever loomed on the horizon and peace seemed as fragile as sand slipping through grasping fingers. <br /><br />The character development of Derfel Cardarn was great. Arthur was amazing and Merlin was magnificent. Bernard Cornwell is an author capable of giving a reader that feeling of elation through masterful story telling. Its no wonder I keep coming back for more.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13911714721762955600" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3204152156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3204152156" />
    <a title="Wick Welker" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53069903-wick-welker"><img alt="Wick Welker" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456500350p2/53069903.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3204152156?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wick Welker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wick Welker" href="/user/show/53069903-wick-welker">Wick Welker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53069903-wick-welker?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53069903-wick-welker?shelf=masterpieces">masterpieces</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53069903-wick-welker?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3204152156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1276684737571871372"><b>An absolute masterpiece of characterization and story telling.</b><br/><br/>Ok... Whoa... This is one of the best books I've read in a really long time. <br/><br/>The tl,dr is that <b>you should move this book up your queue immediately</b> and get ready for some of the best story telling you'll ever experience. Cornwell pulls off a masterful balance of characterization, plot development, pacing, lore and world building. Cornwell achieves that coveted <b>perfect balance of a story that is both driven by plot and characterization </b></span>
  <span id="freeText1276684737571871372" style="display:none"><b>An absolute masterpiece of characterization and story telling.</b><br /><br />Ok... Whoa... This is one of the best books I've read in a really long time. <br /><br />The tl,dr is that <b>you should move this book up your queue immediately</b> and get ready for some of the best story telling you'll ever experience. Cornwell pulls off a masterful balance of characterization, plot development, pacing, lore and world building. Cornwell achieves that coveted <b>perfect balance of a story that is both driven by plot and characterization culminating in a breath-taking resolution.</b><br /><br />The Winter King is told from a first person perspective by an orphan raised within Merlin's estate. With our narrator, Derfel, you will experience not only his growth as an observer and actor in this Arthurian saga, but you'll see an entire cascade of character development among kings, druids, peasants, warriors and Arthur himself. <b>There is beauty, sadness and growth that follows these characters, along with growth of medieval Britain.</b> You'll learn to love and cherish Derfel not only as a narrator, but a man who grows through misery, loss, love, worship and war. You'll come away loving Derfel like he is a real person and not just a character on the page. <br /><br /><b>The characterization of Arthur is truly something to behold.</b> While seeing himself as a pragmatic peace-maker, Arthur tows a very thin line of would-be ruler versus protector of the realm. <b>He is both virtuous and villainous</b> as his peaceful idealism of brotherhood clashes with his unwillingness to give up peripheral power. He believes his actions to be inherently in the best interests of uniting Britain... only if that unity includes himself as a major player. He is heroic and likeable, but also foolish and naive. While he has maintained some civility with his countrymen, his very own foibles lead to the loss of the very men that he wishes to protect. <b>Cornwell has taken the iconic figure of Arthur and molded him into the epic hero full of folly and misguided idealism.</b> He is a truly dynamic character. To quote Arthur himself:<br /><br /><i>"I wanted to do such great things, Derfel, such great things. And in the end it was I who betrayed them, wasn't it?"</i><br /><br />Just to make something clear: <b>this book is brutal toward women.</b> There is sexual assault, sex slavery, servitude and mass objectification of woman everywhere. This book is raw, gritty, violent and stomach churning. All of this brutality toward women is true to the world-building of the dark age era. I did not find it overtly gratuitous or indulgent. In fact, the <b>constant misogyny served to beautifully contrast the incredible women in this book.</b> I'm talking about Nimue. <b>Nimue might be one of my favorite female characters I've ever read.</b> A druid and apprentice of Merlin, this woman grows from a young sorceress into a powerful mage. She suffers every brutalization known to a woman including starving to death on a prison isle. <b>She represents the endurance and adaptability of women living under the unfettered tyranny of male dominion.</b> Salient is this exchange between Nimue and Arthur, emphasizing the misogyny of the man who is supposed to be the hero:<br /><br /><i>"She was an ill-used woman," Arthur said.<br />"All women are," Nimue said.<br />"No,"Arthur insisted. "Maybe most people are, but not all women any more than all men."</i><br /><br /><b>This is a story about changing culture, changing power and changing religions.</b> It is a culture clash of the Britains, the Saxons and the Franks and the boiling over of their different power structures as manifested by their religions. At the forefront is the <b>ever dueling exchange between the old world paganism versus the Christian upstarts.</b> There is <b>deep identity crisis happening over all of nascent England</b> as these forces merge at the nexus of Britain, Arthur and Merlin himself. Is there a magic system in these pages? Perhaps. Or <b>is sorcery just another power structure</b> of the druids, keeping men in check by their superstitions and fear of the unknown?<br /><br /><b>The battles are incredible.</b> Tactics, strategy, bravery and cowardice are the story told with the battles over the heart of Britain. You'll find honer within foes and brotherhood with old enemies. Oath keeping is the fabric of civilization; the breaking of which causes alliances to topple. <br /><br />There's a lot more I can say about what made The Winter King such an incredible story. I highly, highly recommend this to anyone. I will be adding this to my masterpiece shelf and look forward to the sequels. <b>Cornwell is a master.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="1276684737571871372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hUvlqwEsTGdm7Sg4sRGi/idrDRR6lMd8DHm0TvqRqVe/bYx5unKFD8HGnFlG5Kgh8TTq3LQZSRtXB/L/N9JbzQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xi9F/x3CoNqJvo80fJcdaw8ie4qfHvAjCQ7T29ZTcOH8CSwtppxpsi6VO1WLYhe02X2cQlGTfkRScJVqGxCCew==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Conn Iggulden is just such a writer, bringing to vivid life one of the most fascinating eras in human history. In a true masterpiece of historical fiction, Iggulden takes us on a breathtaking journey through ancient Rome, sweeping us into a realm of tyrants and slaves, of dark intrigues and seething passions. What emerges is both a grand romantic tale of coming-of-age in the Roman Empire and a vibrant portrait of the early years of a man who would become the most powerful ruler on earth: Julius Caesar. On the lush Italian peninsula, a new empire is taking shape. At its heart is the city of Rome, a place of glory and decadence, beauty and bloodshed. Against this vivid backdrop, two boys are growing to manhood, dreaming of battles, fame, and glory in service of the mightiest empire the world has ever known. One is the son of a senator, a boy of privilege and ambition to whom much has been given and from whom much is expected. The other is a bastard child, a boy of strength and cunning, whose love for his adoptive family-and his adoptive brother-will be the most powerful force in his life. As young Gaius and Marcus are trained in the art of combat-under the tutelage of one of Rome\'s most fearsome gladiators-Rome itself is being rocked by the art of treachery and ambition, caught in a tug-of-war as two rival generals, Marius and Sulla, push the empire toward civil war. For Marcus, a bloody campaign in Greece will become a young soldier\'s proving ground. For Gaius, the equally deadly infighting of the Roman Senate will be the battlefield where he hones his courage and skill. And for both, the love of an extraordinary slave girl will be an honor each will covet but only one will win. The two friends are forced to walk different paths, and by the time they meet again everything will have changed. Both will have known love, loss, and violence. And the land where they were once innocent will be thrust into the grip of bitter conflict-a conflict that will set Roman against Roman...and put their friendship to the ultimate test. Brilliantly interweaving history and adventure, Conn Iggulden conjures a stunning array of contrasts-from the bloody stench of a battlefield to the opulence of the greatest city in history, from the tenderness of a lover to the treachery of an assassin.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">About Bernard Cornwell</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1504578807p3/12542.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/12542.Bernard_Cornwell">Bernard Cornwell
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
13,028
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13289841202418436742">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden n</span>
  <span id="freeText13289841202418436742" style="display:none">Cornwell was born in London in 1944. His father was a Canadian airman, and his mother, who was English, a member of the Women's Auxiliary Air Force. He was adopted and brought up in Essex by the Wiggins family, who were members of the Peculiar People, a strict Protestant sect who banned frivolity of all kinds and even medicine. After he left them, he changed his name to his birth mother's maiden name, Cornwell.<br /><br />Cornwell was sent away to Monkton Combe School, attended the University of London, and after graduating, worked as a teacher. He attempted to enlist in the British armed services at least three times but was rejected on the grounds of myopia.<br /><br />He then joined BBC's Nationwide and was promoted to become head of current affairs at BBC Northern Ireland. He then joined Thames Television as editor of Thames News. He relocated to the United States in 1980 after marrying an American. Unable to get a green card, he started writing novels, as this did not require a work permit.<br /><br />As a child, Cornwell loved the novels of C.S. Forester, chronicling the adventures of fictional British naval officer Horatio Hornblower during the Napoleonic Wars, and was surprised to find there were no such novels following Lord Wellington's campaign on land. Motivated by the need to support himself in the U.S. through writing, Cornwell decided to write such a series. He named his chief protagonist Richard Sharpe, a rifleman involved in most major battles of the Peninsular War.<br /><br />Cornwell wanted to start the series with the Siege of Badajoz but decided instead to start with a couple of "warm-up" novels. These were <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> and <i>Sharpe's Gold</i>, both published in 1981. <i>Sharpe's Eagle</i> was picked up by a publisher, and Cornwell got a three-book deal. He went on to tell the story of Badajoz in his third Sharpe novel, <i>Sharpe's Company</i>, published in 1982.<br /><br />Cornwell and wife Judy co-wrote a series of novels, published under the pseudonym "Susannah Kells". These were <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, published in 1983, <i>Fallen Angels</i> in 1984, and <i>Coat of Arms</i> (aka <i>The Aristocrats</i>) in 1986. (Cornwell's strict Protestant upbringing informed the background of <i>A Crowning Mercy</i>, which took place during the English Civil War.) In 1987, he also published <i>Redcoat</i>, an American Revolutionary War novel set in Philadelphia during its 1777 occupation by the British.<br /><br />After publishing eight books in his ongoing Sharpe series, Cornwell was approached by a production company interested in adapting them for television. The producers asked him to write a prequel to give them a starting point to the series. They also requested that the story feature a large role for Spanish characters to secure co-funding from Spain. The result was <i>Sharpe’s Rifles</i>, published in 1987, and a series of Sharpe television films staring Sean Bean.<br /><br />A series of contemporary thrillers with sailing as a background and common themes followed: <i>Wildtrack</i> published in 1988, <i>Sea Lord</i> (aka <i>Killer's Wake</i>) in 1989, <i>Crackdown</i> in 1990, <i>Stormchild</i> in 1991, and <i>Scoundrel</i>, a political thriller, in 1992.<br /><br />In June 2006, Cornwell was made an Officer of the Order of the British Empire in the Queen's 80th Birthday Honours List.<br /><br />Cornwell's latest work, <i>Azincourt</i>, was released in the UK in October 2008. The protagonist is an archer who participates in the Battle of Agincourt, another devastating defeat suffered by the French in the Hundred Years War. However, Cornwell has stated that it will not be about Thomas of Hookton from The Grail Quest or any of his relatives.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13289841202418436742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

            if ($('bookCover_68521').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_68521').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>
